The Principle of Mubah Property and Partnership
Ruling with proof that mubah property belongs to the first possessor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that mubah property belongs to the first possessor, explain why partnership in mubah is invalid, and apply the distinction between co-collection and assistance.

Core Idea
Mubah property becomes owned by the first possessor; partnership in it is invalid.

This passage from al-Sharh al-Sameri articulates a fundamental Hanafi principle: ownerless (mubah) property belongs exclusively to the first person to take possession. Consequently, any agreement to form a partnership in such property before possession is legally void. The author provides concrete examples—firewood, grass, hunting—and cites a hadith to support the rule. Understanding this principle is essential for resolving disputes over ownership of natural resources and for correctly structuring contracts involving unowned property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon: gathering firewood, cutting grass, hunting—all forms of taking possession of mubah property. The ruling is that the property becomes owned by the first possessor alone.
The key phrase is "احتطبه فهو له دون صاحبه" (whoever gathers it, it is for him, not his companion).
2
Understand the implication for partnership
The author explicitly states that partnership (shirkah) and agency (wakalah) are invalid in mubah property because ownership vests immediately upon possession. No contract can affect ownership before possession.
This differs from contracts on owned property, where partnership is valid.
3
Examine the dalil (proof) from hadith
The hadith from Ibn Majah compares salt to water: whoever comes to it takes it. This analogy demonstrates that mubah resources belong to the first taker, confirming the rule.
The hadith is ahad (single narration) but considered reliable for this ruling.
4
Apply the distinction between co-collection and assistance
If two people physically collect wood together, each owns half. But if one does the work and the other merely assists, the assistant receives a wage, no ownership share.
This distinction hinges on who actually performs the act of possession (hiyazah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتطبه فهو له دون صاحبه یہ مسئلہ اس قاعدے پر ہے کہ جو چیزیں مباح ہیں اور اس پر جو قبضہ کرلے اسی کی مفت ہو جاتی ہیں۔ ان میں شرکت کی ضرورت نہیں اور نہ ان میں وکالت درست ہے۔ کیونکہ جوں ہی اس پر قبضہ کر لیا وہ چیز اس کی ہو گئی تو دوسرے کی وکالت کی کیا ضرورت ہے؟ مثلا ایندھن کی لکڑی جنگلوں میں مفت ملتی ہے جو اس کو چنے گا اسی کی ہو جائے گی۔ گھاس جنگلوں میں مفت ملتی ہے اس لئے جو اس کو کاٹے گا اس کی ہو جائے گی۔ یہی حال شکار کے جانور کا ہے (۲) حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے کہ جو ان مباح چیزوں پر قبضہ کرے گا اسی کی ہو جائے گی ۔ عن ابيض بـن جـمـال ... فقال رسول الله الله هو منك صدقة وهو (الملح) مثل الماء العد من ورده اخذه (الف) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب اقطاع الانهار والعیون ص ۳۵۵ نمبر ۲۴۷۵) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ نمک وغیرہ پر جو قبضہ کرے گا اسی کا ہو جائے گا۔ اگر دونوں نے ملکر لکڑی چینی تو دونوں کو آدھی آدھی لکڑی ملے گی۔ اور اگر ایک نے لکڑی جمع کی اور دوسرے نے اس کی مدد کی تو مدد کرنے والے کو اس کی اجرت ملے گی اور لکڑی تمام کی تمام جمع کرنے والے کی ہوگی۔ اصول مباح چیزوں میں شرکت صحیح نہیں ہے۔ الاحتطاب : حطب سے مشتق ہے ، جمع کرنا ۔ الاحتشاش : حشیش سے مشتق ہے، گھاس کاٹنا۔ الاصطياد : صید سے مشتق ہے، شکار کرنا۔
Whoever gathers firewood, it is for him, not his companion. This issue is based on the rule that things that are mubah (ownerless), whoever takes possession of them, they become his property free of charge. There is no need for partnership in them, nor is agency valid in them, because as soon as one takes possession, the thing becomes his, so what need is there for agency of another? For example, firewood in forests is free; whoever collects it, it becomes his. Grass in forests is free; whoever cuts it, it becomes his. The same applies to hunting animals. (2) There is proof in hadith that whoever takes possession of these mubah things, they become his. From Abyad ibn Jamal... The Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) said: 'It is a charity from you, and salt is like water that one who finds it takes it.' (Ibn Majah, chapter on grants of rivers and springs, p. 355, no. 2475). This hadith indicates that salt, etc., whoever takes possession of it, it becomes his. If both of them gather wood together, then each gets half the wood. And if one collected the wood and the other assisted him, then the assistant gets his wage, and all the wood belongs to the collector. Principle: Partnership in mubah things is not valid. Al-ihṭṭāb: derived from ḥaṭab, meaning gathering. Al-iḥtishāsh: derived from ḥashīsh, meaning cutting grass. Al-iṣṭiyād: derived from ṣayd, meaning hunting.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage establishes the Hanafi ruling that ownerless (mubah) property becomes the exclusive property of the first person to take possession. Therefore, partnership in such property is invalid because ownership is immediate and individual. The author provides examples: firewood, grass, and hunting. He supports this with a hadith from Ibn Majah regarding salt mines. The hadith indicates that salt, like water, belongs to whoever first takes possession. The passage also clarifies that if two people gather wood together, they each own half; but if one does the work and the other merely assists, the assistant is entitled only to a wage, not a share of the wood. The fundamental principle is that mubah assets cannot be subject to partnership. The author then defines key terms used in the chapter: iḥtṭāb (gathering wood), iḥtishāsh (cutting grass), and iṣṭiyād (hunting).

Key Terms
احتطاب (iḥṭṭāb): Gathering firewood
احتشاش (iḥtishāsh): Cutting grass
اصطياد (iṣṭiyād): Hunting
مباح (mubāḥ): Ownerless property that is not subject to ownership rights until possession

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if two people together gather firewood from a forest, how is the ownership of the wood divided?
A) Both own the wood equally because they worked together.
B) The person who first touches the wood owns it all.
C) Both own it equally only if they both physically collected it; if one assisted, he gets a wage.
D) The wood belongs to all people in common.

Key Takeaway
Mubah property becomes owned exclusively by the first person to take possession; any partnership contract on mubah property before possession is void.

Definition of Wakalah and Its Proofs from Quran and Sunnah
Definition and textual proofs for wakalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of wakalah in Hanafi fiqh and identify the Quranic verse and hadith that establish its permissibility.

Core Idea
Wakalah is the delegation of a permissible act to another; its legitimacy is proven by the Quranic story of the Cave Companions and the Prophet's appointment of Ali as an agent.

This lesson introduces the contract of wakalah (agency) as defined by the Hanafi school. You will learn the precise meaning of wakalah and the primary textual evidence – a Quranic verse and a prophetic hadith – that form the basis for its validity. Understanding these proofs is essential before exploring the detailed rulings of agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its definition
The act is appointing another to act on one's behalf. The author defines wakalah as delegation: not doing the task oneself but making another an agent.
Definition often includes 'permissible act' as a condition.
2
Locate the dalil
Two proofs are cited: a Quranic verse (Surah al-Kahf 18:19) and a hadith from Bukhari narrated by Ali.
The verse shows a group sending one of them to buy food; the hadith shows the Prophet appointing Ali to slaughter camels and give charity.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In the verse, 'Send one of you' (ابعثوا احدكم) indicates delegation of authority. In the hadith, the Prophet's command to Ali demonstrates appointment for an act.
Both involve delegating a task that the principal could do themselves.
4
Note the hukm level
Wakalah is ja'iz (permissible) – a valid contract but not obligatory. All schools agree on its permissibility.
The ruling is based on these proofs; no obligation to appoint an agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة

ضروری نوٹ وکالت کے معنی سپرد کرنا ، خود کوئی کام نہ کرے اور دوسرے کو کام کرنے کا وکیل بنائے اس کو وکالت کہتے ہیں۔ اس کا ثبوت اس آیت میں ہے۔ بابعثوا احدكم بورقكم هذه الى المدينة فلينظر ايها از كى طعاما فليأتكم برزق منه (الف) ( آیت ۱۹ سورۃ الکہف (۱۸) اس آیت میں اصحاب کہف کے ساتھیوں نے کھانا خریدنے کا وکیل بنایا ہے (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن علی قال امرنی رسول الله الا الله ان اتصدق بجلال البدن التي نحرت وبجلودها (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب و کالۃ الشریک الشریک فی القسمۃ وغیر ھاص ۳۰۸ نمبر ۲۲۹۹) اس حدیث میں اونٹ ذبح کرنے اور اس کی کھال کو صدقہ کرنے کا وکیل حضرت علی کو آپ نے بنایا۔
Book of Agency

Necessary Note: The meaning of wakalah is to delegate, i.e., not to do a task oneself but to appoint another as an agent to do it. Its proof is in this verse: 'Send one of you with this silver of yours to the city, and let him look for the purest food, and bring you provision from it' (Surah al-Kahf 18:19). In this verse, the Companions of the Cave appointed someone as an agent to buy food. (2) In the hadith: 'Ali said: The Messenger of Allah commanded me to give in charity the humps and skins of the camels that I slaughtered.' (Bukhari, Chapter on Agency of a partner in division etc., no. 2299). In this hadith, the Prophet appointed Ali as an agent to slaughter the camels and give their skins in charity.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This is the introductory section of the Book of Wakalah (Agency). The author defines wakalah as entrusting a task to another, meaning the principal does not do it himself but appoints an agent (wakil) to do it. The legitimacy of agency is established from two primary sources: (1) The Quranic verse from Surat al-Kahf (18:19), where the Companions of the Cave appointed one of them to go to the city and purchase food. This is considered a proof (dalil) for the permissibility of appointing an agent for buying and selling. (2) The hadith narrated by Ali (radiyallahu anhu) that the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) appointed him to slaughter camels and give their skins in charity. This shows that the Prophet appointed an agent for an act he himself could perform. These texts establish the validity of agency in Islamic law. The Hanafi school holds that wakalah is a valid contract with specific rules.

Key Terms
وکالت (wakalah): The contract of appointing another to perform a permissible act on one's behalf.
وکیل (wakil): The person appointed as agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse is cited as evidence for the permissibility of wakalah?
A) Surah al-Baqarah 2:282
B) Surah al-Kahf 18:19
C) Surah al-Nisa 4:29
D) Surah al-Ma'idah 5:2

Key Takeaway
Wakalah is a valid contract in Hanafi fiqh, proven by the Quranic verse of the Cave Companions and the hadith of Ali's appointment.

General Principle: Any Permissible Contract Can Be Delegated
Ruling on delegability of contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi principle of delegation and apply it to determine which contracts can be delegated.

Core Idea
Any contract you can validly conclude yourself, you can delegate to an agent – delegation is the default rule.

This lesson explores the foundational principle of wakalah (agency) in Hanafi fiqh. The source text states a general rule: any contract that a person may validly conclude himself, it is permissible for him to appoint an agent for it. This principle establishes the permissibility of delegation as the default for all permissible contracts, unless there is a specific prohibition. Understanding this principle is key to grasping the scope of agency in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the legal act: delegating a contract to an agent. The ruling is ja'iz (mubah) – permissible.
The principle applies to all contracts, not just sale or purchase.
2
Identify the condition
The key condition: the principal himself must be able to validly conclude that contract. If the principal lacks capacity (e.g., minor, insane), delegation is not valid.
Capacity includes legal majority and sanity.
3
Note the scope
The principle covers all types of contracts: sale, lease, marriage, litigation, etc. It also includes agency for recovering rights and proving them.
The principle is broad; exceptions exist but are rare.
4
Consider examples from evidence
The Prophet (saw) delegated slaughter of camels and charity to Ali (ra), and the Companions of the Cave delegated food purchase. Both are valid delegations because the principals could do these acts themselves.
These examples confirm the principle in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٣٧٠ (١) كل عقد جاز ان يعقده الانسان بنفسه جاز ان يوكل به غیره [ ۱۳۷۱ (۲) ]
ويجوز التوكيل بالخصومة في سائر الحقوق وباثباتها ويجوز بالاستيفاء.

کتاب الوكالة )

ضروری نوٹ وکالت کے معنی سپرد کرنا ، خود کوئی کام نہ کرے اور دوسرے کو کام کرنے کا وکیل بنائے اس کو وکالت کہتے ہیں۔ اس کا ثبوت اس آیت میں ہے۔ بابعثوا احدكم بورقكم هذه الى المدينة فلينظر ايها از كى طعاما فليأتكم برزق منه (الف) ( آیت ۱۹ سورۃ الکہف (۱۸) اس آیت میں اصحاب کہف کے ساتھیوں نے کھانا خریدنے کا وکیل بنایا ہے (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن علی قال امرنی رسول الله الا الله ان اتصدق بجلال البدن التي نحرت وبجلودها (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب و کالۃ الشریک الشریک فی القسمۃ وغیر ھاص ۳۰۸ نمبر ۲۲۹۹) اس حدیث میں اونٹ ذبح کرنے اور اس کی کھال کو صدقہ کرنے کا وکیل حضرت علی کو آپ نے بنایا۔
(۱۳۷۰) (۱) ہر وہ عقد جو انسان خود کر سکتا ہو، جائز ہے کہ اس کا دوسرے کو بھی وکیل بنائے۔
ا جو کام خود کر سکتا ہے اس کام کے کرنے کا دوسروں کو بھی وکیل بنا سکتا ہے۔
(1) اوپر کی حدیث میں حضور نے حضرت علی کو اونٹ ذبح کرنے اور کھال صدقہ کرنے کا وکیل بنایا۔ اور یہ کام حضور خود بھی کر سکتے تھے (۲) بعض مرتبہ آدمی خود ایک کام نہیں کر سکتا ہے تو مجبوری ہوتی ہے کہ دوسروں سے وہ کام کروائے (۳) اوپر کی آیت میں بھی کہف کے ساتھیوں نے دوسرے کو کھانا خریدنے کا وکیل بنایا ہے۔
[1370] (1) Every contract that a person may validly conclude himself, it is permissible for him to appoint another as agent for it. [1371] (2) And it is permissible to appoint an agent for litigation in all rights, and for proving them, and for recovery. Book of Agency. Necessary Note: ... (repeated from previous lesson) ... [1370] (1) Every contract that a person may validly conclude himself, it is permissible for him to appoint another as agent for it. He can appoint others for a task that he can do himself. (1) In the above hadith, the Prophet appointed Ali to slaughter the camel and give the skin in charity, and the Prophet himself could have done it. (2) Sometimes a person cannot do a task himself, so there is a need to get it done by others. (3) In the above verse, the Companions of the Cave appointed another to buy food.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents the foundational principle of wakalah: any contract that a person is legally capable of performing himself, he may delegate to an agent. The rule is 'whatever you can do, you can delegate.' The commentator supports this with the hadith of Ali and the Quranic verse from the previous lesson. The principle includes both voluntary acts (like charity) and contractual acts (like buying and selling). It also introduces that agency is valid for litigation (khusumah), proof (ithbat), and recovery (istifa'). This shows that wakalah covers a wide range of legal activities. The Hanafi school uses this general rule to permit agency unless there is a specific prohibition.

Key Terms
وکالت (wakalah): Agency; the contract of deputyship.
عقد (aqd): A contract or legal transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general principle, for which types of contracts can one appoint an agent?
A) Only for contracts of sale.
B) Only for contracts that the principal cannot do himself.
C) Any contract that the principal himself may validly conclude.
D) Only for contracts that are obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The general principle: any contract a person can validly conclude himself may be delegated to an agent – permission is the default.

Agency for Litigation, Proof, and Recovery
Ruling on agency for litigation, proof, and recovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three types of permissible agency in Hanafi law (litigation, proof, recovery) and explain the evidence from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
You can appoint someone to plead your case, prove your right, and recover it — agency in litigation is the norm, not the exception.

This passage clarifies that agency is not limited to buying and selling; it extends to the courtroom. Hanafi jurisprudence allows you to authorize another person to litigate on your behalf, present evidence, and receive what is owed to you. The reason is practical: not everyone is skilled in legal argumentation, and the Prophet himself appointed a representative in a dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of agency
The passage mentions agency for litigation (khusumah), proof (ithbat), and recovery (istifa'). Understand the definition of each: litigation includes presenting the case, proof means establishing the right, and recovery is receiving it after judgment.
Litigation agency is the most comprehensive.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason given is practical: not everyone has the ability to present a case well before a judge. This establishes a need-based permissibility.
Necessity (hajah) is a recognized basis for permissibility in Hanafi law.
3
Examine the prophetic evidence
The author cites the hadith of Thabit bin Qays, whom the Prophet appointed to respond to Musaylimah al-Kadhdhab. This shows that verbal representation in legal matters is sanctioned by Sunnah.
The hadith is from Bukhari (Maghazi), a sound source.
4
Apply to modern contexts
This ruling directly applies to hiring a lawyer. The lawyer acts as an agent for litigation, proof, and recovery. The same conditions apply: the agent must be competent and act within the scope of delegation.
Modern legal systems often recognize this as 'representation by counsel.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز التوكيل بالخصومة في سائر الحقوق وباثباتها ويجوز بالاستيفاء.

(۱۳۷۱) (۲) اور جائز ہے وکیل بنانا تمام حقوق میں جھگڑا کرنے کا اور ان کے ثابت کرنے کا اور جائز ہے حقوق حاصل کرنے کے لئے۔ تمام حقوق میں خصومت کرنے کا وکیل بنا سکتا ہے۔ خصومت کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ قاضی کے سامنے اچھے انداز میں مقدمہ پیش کرے، پھر اس کو ثابت کرے، گواہ پیش کرے اور اپنے حق میں فیصلہ کے لئے زور لگائے۔ ان تمام کاروائیوں کو وکیل بالخصومت کہتے ہیں۔ اسی طرح حق کو ثابت کرنے اور حق کو وصول کرنے کے لئے بھی وکیل بنا سکتا ہے۔
(۱) ہر آدمی قاضی کے سامنے اچھے انداز میں مقدمہ پیش کرنے کی اہلیت نہیں رکھتا، اس لئے خصومت کا وکیل بنانا جائز ہے (۲) حضور نے مسیلمہ کذاب کو جواب دینے کے سلسلے میں ثابت بن قیس کو وکیل بنایا ہے۔ اس لمبی حدیث کا ٹکڑا پیش خدمت ہے۔ عن ابن عباس قال قدم مسيلمة الكذاب على عهد النبي الا الله ... وهذا ثابت بن قيس يجيبك عنى ثم انصرف عنه (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب وفد بنی حنیفہ و حدیث ثمامہ بن اعمال، کتاب المغازی ص ۶۲۷ نمبر ۴۳۷۳) (۳) مقدمہ پیش کرنے کے لئے عبد الرحمن بن سہل آگے حاشیہ : (الف) تم میں سے ایک کو ان سکوں کو لیک شہر بھیجیں تو وہ دیکھے کہ کون سا کھانا پاک ہے تو اس سے کچھ کھانے کا لائے (ب ) حضور نے مجھے حکم دیا کہ اونٹ کی جل کو صدقہ کروں جس کو میں نے ذبح کیا ہے اور اس کی کھال کو صدقہ کروں (ج) مسیلمہ کذاب حضور کے زمانے میں آیا۔ یہ ثابت بن قیس ہیں، یہ میری جانب سے تم کو جواب دیں گے، پھر آپ واپس چلے آئے۔
And it is permissible to appoint an agent for litigation in all rights, and for proving them, and for recovery. [1371] (2) And it is permissible to appoint an agent for disputing in all rights, and for proving them, and permissible for obtaining rights. One can appoint an agent for litigation in all rights. The meaning of litigation is to present the case well before the judge, then prove it, bring witnesses, and strive for a decision in one's favor. All these procedures are called litigation agency. Similarly, one can appoint an agent for proving a right and for receiving it. (1) Not every person has the ability to present a case well before the judge, therefore appointing a litigation agent is permissible. (2) The Prophet appointed Thabit bin Qays to respond to Musaylimah al-Kadhdhab. A portion of this long hadith: Ibn Abbas said: 'Musaylimah al-Kadhdhab came during the time of the Prophet... This is Thabit bin Qays, he will answer you on my behalf, then he departed.' (Bukhari, Chapter on the delegation of Banu Hanifah and hadith of Thumamah bin Uthal, Maghazi, p. 627, no. 4373).
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This ruling elaborates on the types of agency permissible. Specifically, one may appoint an agent for khusumah (litigation) – meaning to plead one's case before a judge, to prove one's right (ithbat) by presenting evidence and witnesses, and to recover (istifa') the right after judgment. The reason given is practical: not everyone is adept at presenting cases in court. The commentator supports this with the hadith from Bukhari where the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) appointed Thabit bin Qays as his representative to respond to the impostor Musaylimah al-Kadhdhab. This shows the permissibility of appointing an agent for defense and litigation. The Hanafi school thus permits agency in all legal proceedings concerning rights, including claims and defenses.

Key Terms
خصومة (khusumah): Litigation; presenting a case before a judge, including argument and proof.
استيفاء (istifa'): Recovery or receipt of a right after it has been established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical reason for permitting agency in litigation?
A) To save time.
B) Because not everyone can present a case well before a judge.
C) Because the Quran commands it.
D) To avoid personal confrontation.

Key Takeaway
Agency for litigation, proof, and recovery is a recognized and necessary aspect of Hanafi law, supported by prophetic practice.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Wakalah in Litigation Without Opponent's Consent
Intra-Hanafi dispute on condition of opponent's consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on opponent's consent for wakalah in litigation, explain the legal reasoning behind each view, and identify the practical consequence of the dispute.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa requires opponent's consent for litigation wakalah; Sahibayn allow it without consent.

In this lesson, we examine a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when does a principal need the opponent's consent to appoint a wakil for litigation? Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ on this point. The dispute hinges on whether the harm to the opponent or the principal's right to delegate should take precedence. By understanding this difference, you will see how the Hanafi school accommodates multiple valid juristic perspectives.

Guided Path
1
State the position of Imam Abu Hanifa
Identify the exact ruling of Abu Hanifa: wakalah in litigation is not valid without the opponent's consent, except when the principal is sick or absent on a journey of three days or more.
2
State the position of the Sahibayn
Identify the opposing view: wakalah in litigation is valid without the opponent's consent, based on the principal's right to delegate.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
Analyze the athar of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, who appointed 'Aqil as his wakil without apparent necessity or opponent's consent, as recorded by Imam al-Bayhaqi.
This athar is cited to show a precedent without consent.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Understand Abu Hanifa's concern: a wakil may cause harm to the opponent by his conduct or argument, so the opponent's consent is needed to avoid that harm. He focuses on harm to the opponent.
The harm is potential, not actual.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, one must obtain opponent's consent unless the principal is excused (sick or traveling). Under Sahibayn's view, no consent is needed. However, if a wakil is appointed without consent and a decision is rendered, it is valid.
This affects how litigation is conducted in practice.

Source Text

(1373) (4) Imam Abu Hanifa said: Wakalah in litigation is not permissible except with the consent of the opponent, unless the principal is sick or absent on a journey of three days or more. Footnote: (a) When 'Abd al-Rahman began to speak, the Prophet said, 'The elder, the elder' because he was the youngest, so he remained silent and the elders spoke. (b) 'Ali disliked litigation; when he had a case, he would appoint 'Aqil as wakil; when 'Aqil grew old, he appointed me. (c) The Prophet said, 'Avert hudud from the Muslims as much as you can...' [Page 282] Al-Sharh al-Thamiri vol. 2, p. 277, Kitab al-Wakalah. '...litigation except with the consent of the opponent, unless the principal is sick or absent on a journey of three days or more.' 1374 (5) Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Wakalah is permissible without the consent of the opponent. They say that people differ in their conduct; it is possible that the wakil may cause unnecessary harm to the opponent and create difficulty in obtaining a just decision. Therefore, without necessity or consent, appointing a wakil for litigation is not permissible. If he appoints and the decision is given, it will be valid. (1374) (5) And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Appointing a wakil without the opponent's consent is permissible. And an athar has passed that 'Ali used to appoint 'Aqil as wakil without any necessity. From 'Abdullah bin Ja'far: 'Ali disliked litigation; so when he had a case, he would appoint 'Aqil; when 'Aqil grew old, he appointed me.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, vol. 6, p. 134, no. 11437). This shows that even without necessity and without the opponent's consent, one can appoint a wakil for litigation. (2) They say that appointing a wakil is the principal's own right, so it is not dependent on the opponent's consent; rather, one can appoint a wakil in litigation without his consent. There is a principle: Abu Hanifa looks at the harm to the opponent, while the Sahibayn look at the harm to the principal himself.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding wakalah in litigation (khusumat). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that appointing a wakil for litigation is not valid without the opponent's consent, except when the principal is sick or absent on a journey of three days or more. His reasoning, as explained in the commentary, is that people have different capabilities; a wakil may cause harm to the opponent by his style of argument and may obstruct the true decision. Therefore, the opponent's consent is necessary to prevent this potential harm. However, if the principal is unable to appear (due to sickness or travel), consent is not required because the principal has a necessity. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree. They hold that wakalah in litigation is permissible even without the opponent's consent. They support this with the athar of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, who used to appoint 'Aqil as his wakil without any apparent necessity and without the opponent's consent. They argue that appointing a wakil is the principal's own right; it is an extension of his own capacity to litigate. The principle underlying this difference is that Abu Hanifa focuses on the potential harm to the opponent, while the Sahibayn focus on the harm to the principal if he is forced to litigate personally. The text also notes that if the appointment is made without consent and a decision is reached, it is valid (i.e., the ruling is effective). This is a classic difference in legal methodology: Abu Hanifa considers the external effects (mafsada) on the opponent, while the Sahibayn consider the principal's autonomy.

Key Terms
خصومة (khusumat): Litigation or legal dispute in court.
رضا (rida): Consent of the opposing party to the appointment of a wakil.
مسيرة ثلاثة ايام (masiratu thalathati ayyam): A journey of three days' distance, which qualifies as a valid excuse for absence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the wakil is a relative
B) When the principal is sick or on a journey of three days or more
C) When the opponent is also absent
D) It is never permissible without consent

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's internal dispute on opponent's consent in litigation wakalah illustrates how different maqasid (preventing harm vs. facilitating delegation) lead to different rulings.

Condition for Valid Wakalah: Principal's Legal Capacity
Ruling on condition of principal's capacity for wakalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid wakalah regarding the principal's capacity and explain why a minor or insane person cannot appoint an agent.

Core Idea
A principal must be legally capable of the act he delegates; otherwise, the wakalah is invalid.

In this passage, we learn a foundational condition for the validity of wakalah (agency) in Hanafi fiqh. The principal (muwakkil) must be a person who is himself capable of performing the act for which he appoints an agent. This capacity requires sanity, adulthood, and freedom. The text cites a hadith to support that minors and the insane are not legally responsible, hence cannot delegate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Read the first sentence: the principal must be one who can dispose of property and upon whom obligations are binding. This is the core condition.
Condition is two-fold: capacity to perform the act and being subject to Shari'ah rulings.
2
Break down the requirements
The passage explains that the principal must be sane, adult (baligh), and free (hur). These are the three elements of legal capacity (ahliyyat al-ada' kamilah).
Freedom is mentioned; a slave lacks full capacity in classical Hanafi law.
3
Connect to the hadith proof
The hadith 'The pen is lifted from three' shows that minors and insane persons are not held legally responsible (taklif). Since wakalah involves legal responsibility, they cannot appoint a wakil.
The hadith is cited with reference to Tirmidhi; its transmission is ahad but accepted in fiqh.
4
Apply to concrete cases
Identify who is excluded: a sleeping person (until awake), a child (until mature), a mentally deficient person (until sane). None of these can be principals.
The condition applies to the act being delegated; if the principal cannot do the act himself, he cannot delegate it.
5
State the ruling
If the principal lacks any of these capacities (sanity, adulthood, freedom), the wakalah is invalid (ghayr sahih). The agent cannot be bound by the contract.
This is a widely agreed condition across schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۳۷۵] (٦) ومن شرط الوكالة ان يكون الموكل ممن يملك التصرف ويلزمه وکالت کی شرطوں میں ہے یہ ہے کہ خود وکیل بنانے والا جس چیز کا وکیل بنارہا ہو اس کام کو کر سکتا ہو۔ اگر وہ خود نہیں کر سکتا ہے تو وہ دوسروں کو کرنے کا حکم کیسے دے گا ؟ اور دوسری شرط یہ ہے کہ شریعت کے احکام اس پر لازم ہوتے ہوں یعنی وہ خود عاقل، بالغ اور آزاد ہو۔ اگر وہ عاقل، بالغ اور آزاد نہیں ہے تو اس پر شریعت کے احکام لازم نہیں ہونگے ۔ جب اس پر لازم نہیں ہوتے تو دوسرے کو احکام لازم کرنے کا حکم کیسے دے؟ یعنی اس کام کو کرنے کا حکم کیسے دے سکتا ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے نابالغ اور مجنون سے احکام انہ کیئے گئے ہیں۔ عن علی ان رسول الله الله قال رفع القلم عن ثلاثة عن النائم حتى يستيقظ وعن الصبر حتى يشب وعن المعتوه حتى يعقل (ب) ( ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء فیمن لا یجب علیہ الحد ۔ حضرت علی مقدم با من بات تھے۔ پس ان کے لئے کوئی مقدمہ ہوتا تو وہ اس میں عقیل بن ابی طالب کو وکیل بناتے ۔ پس جب حضرت عقیل بوڑھے ، مجھے وکیل بنانے لگے (ب )آپ نے فرمایا تین آدمیوں سے قلم اٹھا دیا گیا ہے سونے والے سے جب تک بیدار نہ ہو جائے اور بچے سے جب تک بالغ نہ ہوجائے اور منقہ سے جب تک عقل والا نہ ہو جائے۔
(6) Among the conditions of wakalah is that the principal should be one who possesses the capacity to dispose of property and upon whom obligations are binding. Among the conditions of wakalah is that the person appointing the wakil should himself be able to do the act for which he is appointing the wakil. If he himself cannot do it, how can he command another to do it? The second condition is that the rulings of Shari'ah are binding upon him, i.e., he should be sane, adult, and free. If he is not sane, adult, and free, then the rulings of Shari'ah are not binding upon him. When they are not binding upon him, how can he command another to be bound by rulings? i.e., how can he command that act to be done? The hadith states: The minor and the insane have been relieved of (the obligation of) rulings. From 'Ali: The Messenger of Allah said, 'The pen is lifted from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he becomes mature, and from the mentally deficient until he becomes sane.' (Tirmidhi, Bab Ma Ja'a fiman la Yajibu 'alayhi al-Hadd). 'Ali disliked litigation; when he had a case, he would appoint 'Aqil as wakil; when 'Aqil grew old, he appointed me. He said: 'The pen is lifted from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he becomes mature, and from the mentally deficient until he becomes sane.'
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage states a condition for the validity of wakalah: the principal (muwakkil) must be legally capable of performing the act for which he appoints an agent. Specifically, he must be a person who can validly dispose of property (yamluk al-tasarruf) and is subject to the binding rulings of Shari'ah (yalzimuhu). In Urdu commentary, it explains that the principal must be sane, adult (baligh), and free (hur). If he is not, e.g., a minor or insane, he cannot delegate because the delegated act requires legal capacity. The hadith of the Prophet (رفع القلم عن ثلاثة) is cited to show that minors and the insane are not held responsible for legal obligations, hence cannot appoint an agent for such matters. The passage also repeats the athar of 'Ali appointing 'Aqil, but this appears to be a separate note. The ruling is clear: wakalah is only valid if the principal has legal capacity to perform the act himself.

Key Terms
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent (wakil).
تصرف (tasarruf): Legal disposition of property or rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The principal must be present at the contract
C) The principal must be sane, adult, and free
D) The principal must be a male

Key Takeaway
The principal in a wakalah contract must be legally capable (sane, adult, free) of performing the delegated act, as per the hadith of the three from whom the pen is lifted.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Opponent's Consent in Litigation Agency
Intra-Hanafi dispute on opponent's consent in litigation agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state both Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's views on opponent's consent in litigation agency and identify which position is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school does not require opponent's consent for litigation agency; the mufta bihi follows the Sahibayn.

This passage presents a famous intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a principal must obtain the opponent's consent before appointing an agent to litigate. Imam Abu Hanifa required it except in certain circumstances, while the two companions (Sahibayn) allowed it unconditionally, citing the practice of Ali ibn Abi Talib. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies how later Hanafi jurists prioritized practicality over theoretical harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed condition
The act is appointing an agent for litigation (wakalah bi al-khusumat). The disputed condition is the opponent's consent (rida al-khasm). Determine what each imam requires.
The condition is external to the contract; it relates to the validity of the agency in the context of litigation.
2
State each imam's position
Abu Hanifa: The agency is not valid without the opponent's consent, unless the principal is sick or absent for three days' travel or more. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): The agency is valid without the opponent's consent.
Note the exception in Abu Hanifa's view; it softens the condition.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's evidence
They cite the practice of Ali ibn Abi Talib, who appointed Aqeel ibn Abi Talib and later Abdullah ibn Ja'far as agents without obtaining the opponent's consent. This serves as a dalil naqli.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Muhtadi; its authenticity is accepted by the Sahibayn.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, if the opponent does not consent, the agency is invalid unless the principal is sick or far away. Under the Sahibayn, the agency is valid regardless. This affects whether legal proceedings can proceed through an agent.
The practical consequence is significant for access to representation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is the view of the Sahibayn. It is more practical and supported by established practice.
This is a clear example of a later tarjih within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالخصومة الا برضا الخصم الا ان يكون الموكل مريضا او غائبا مسيرة ثلاثة ايام فصاعدا وقال ابو يوسف و محمد رحمهما الله يجوز التوكيل بغير رضا الخصم عن عبد الله بن جعفر قال كان على طالب يكره الخصمة فكان اذا كانت له خصومة وكل فيها عقيل بن ابي طالب فلما كبر عقيل وكلني
Litigation agency is not permissible without the opponent's consent unless the principal is sick or absent a distance of three days' travel or more. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: Appointing an agent without the opponent's consent is permissible. From Abdullah ibn Ja'far: 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) disliked disputes, so whenever he had a case he would appoint Aqeel ibn Abi Talib as his agent, and when Aqeel grew old he appointed me.'
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the permissibility of appointing an agent for litigation (wakalah bi al-khusumat) without the consent of the opposing party. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that such agency is not valid unless the principal is sick or traveling three days away, as the agent's skill may harm the opponent. However, Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that it is permissible without consent, citing the practice of Ali ibn Abi Talib who appointed agents for his cases. The hadith from Abdullah ibn Ja'far is used to support the Sahibayn's position. The commentary explains that Ali disliked arguments and would delegate representation. Thus, the operative ruling in the Hanafi school follows the Sahibayn's view on this issue.

Key Terms
وكالة بالخصومة (wakāla bil-khuṣūma): Agency specifically for litigation, where the principal appoints an agent to represent him in court or dispute.
رضا الخصم (riḍā al-khaṣm): Consent of the opposing party in litigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position does not require opponent's consent for litigation agency, following the Sahibayn's view.

Condition of the Principal: Capability and Accountability for Valid Agency
States the principal's capacity condition for valid agency with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two requirements for a principal in a valid agency contract and explain the proof from the hadith.

Core Idea
A principal in agency must be legally capable of disposal and accountable; the non-accountable cannot delegate.

This lesson introduces a foundational condition for the validity of an agency contract (wakalah). The principal (al-muwakkil) must have the legal capacity to perform the act himself and be subject to the binding rulings of Sharia. A clear proof from the Sunnah establishes that children and the insane are not accountable, thus cannot appoint agents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Read the first part of the passage: 'Among the conditions of agency is that the principal be among those who have the authority to dispose and are bound by rulings.' This sets out two linked requirements.
2
Understand the two requirements
First, the principal must have legal authority over the matter (e.g., he can sell his own property). Second, he must be accountable (mukallaf) under Sharia, meaning he is obliged to follow rulings.
The second requirement implies sanity and maturity.
3
Examine the proof
The hadith from Ali (RA) states that the pen is lifted from three: the sleeper, the child (until puberty), and the insane (until sane). This means they are not held responsible for their actions.
The word 'al-sabr' in the text is likely a typo for 'al-sabi' (the child).
4
Apply the ruling
Reason: if a person is not accountable for his own actions, he cannot delegate an action to another. Therefore, a child or insane person cannot appoint an agent, even if they are physically able to perform the act.
Contrast: a sane adult who is asleep temporarily cannot appoint an agent while asleep, but when awake he can.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن شرط الوكالة ان يكون الموكل ممن يملك التصرف ويلزمه الاحكام عن علي ان رسول الله الله قال رفع القلم عن ثلاثة عن النائم حتى يستيقظ وعن الصبر حتى يشب وعن المعتوه حتى يعقل
From the conditions of agency is that the principal be among those who have the authority to dispose (of property) and are bound by rulings. From Ali that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: The pen is lifted from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he reaches puberty, and from the insane until he becomes sane.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage outlines a key condition for the principal in a valid agency contract: he must be legally capable of performing the action himself (mimman yamlik al-tasarruf) and be subject to the binding rulings of the Sharia (wa yalzimuhu al-ahkam). This means the principal must be an adult of sound mind. The hadith cited (from Ali, but also in Tirmidhi) explains that children and the insane are not held accountable for their actions, hence they cannot appoint agents. The commentary elaborates that a child or insane person cannot appoint an agent because they themselves are not bound by rulings; likewise, a person cannot delegate an action he cannot personally perform (e.g., a man cannot delegate the divorce of a stranger's wife).

Key Terms
يملك التصرف (yamlik al-taṣarruf): Having the legal authority to dispose of property and enter into transactions.
يلزمه الاحكام (yalzimuhu al-aḥkām): Being subject to the legal rulings of the Sharia, meaning the person is accountable (mukallaf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) A sane adult female
C) A discerning minor (sabi mumayyiz)
D) A free person with full capacity

Key Takeaway
For a valid agency, the principal must be legally capable and accountable; children and insane persons cannot appoint agents.

Condition of the Agent: Understanding and Intent
States that the agent must understand and intend the transaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid agent in Hanafi law and identify which persons qualify.

Core Idea
An agent must understand the transaction and intend it; without both, agency is void.

This lesson examines a foundational condition from the Hanafi law of agency (wakala). The source passage states that the agent must be among those who 'understand sale and purchase' and 'intend it.' We will unpack what these terms mean and why they are essential for a valid agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the ruling
The subject is the agent (wakil) in a contract of agency. The ruling is that it is a condition (shart) for the validity of the agency that the agent understands the transaction and intends it.
2
Define 'understanding' (ya'qil al-bay')
The agent must have mental capacity to comprehend the nature of the transaction. This excludes insane persons and non-discerning children. A discerning minor (sabi mumayyiz) may qualify if he understands.
The Hanafi school allows a mumayyiz child to be an agent for simple transactions.
3
Define 'intent' (yaqṣiduhu)
The agent must deliberately intend the transaction, not act jokingly or without seriousness. Mere utterance without intent is insufficient.
Intent (qasd) is a separate condition from understanding.
4
Apply the condition to a scenario
Given a potential agent, check: (1) Does he understand the transaction? (2) Does he intend it? If both yes, he qualifies. If not, agency is invalid.
The burden is on the principal to appoint a qualified agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوكيل ممن يعقل البيع ويقصده
And the agent (must be) among those who understand sale and purchase and intend it.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage states the condition for the agent (wakil) in an agency contract: he must be someone who understands the nature of the transaction (here sale and purchase are mentioned as examples) and has the deliberate intent (qasd) to perform the act. The commentary explains that the agent must be sane and mature, or at least a discerning child who can comprehend buying and selling. He must not consider it a joke or play. The hadith that the pen is lifted from children is relevant, indicating that children who do not understand are not suitable agents. Thus, the agent must have the mental capacity to understand the transaction and the volition to execute it.

Key Terms
يعقل البيع (ya'qil al-bay'): Understands the concept of sale and purchase, i.e., has the mental capacity to grasp the transaction.
يقصده (yaqṣiduhu): Intends the transaction deliberately, not as a joke or without seriousness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A mature, sane adult who understands car purchases
B) A discerning child who knows what buying a car means
C) An adult who is insane and does not understand transactions
D) A responsible adult with experience in sales

Key Takeaway
For an agency to be valid, the agent must both understand the transaction and deliberately intend it.

Who Can Appoint an Agent: Free Adults and Authorized Slaves
Ruling on valid principals for agency (wakala)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two categories of principals who can validly appoint an agent and identify the condition for the agent's capacity.

Core Idea
A free adult or an authorized slave can appoint an agent of their own kind.

This lesson examines a foundational rule in Hanafi agency law: who is qualified to act as a principal in appointing an agent. The text states a simple but important principle: a free adult and an authorized slave (ma'dhun) may each appoint someone like themselves. Understanding this rule clarifies the capacity requirements for both parties in a wakala contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of principals
The text mentions 'al-hurr al-baligh' (the free adult) and 'al-ma'dhun' (the authorized slave/child). Note that the ma'dhun has been granted permission by his master or guardian to engage in trade.
The ma'dhun is not fully independent but has transactional capacity by permission.
2
Interpret the phrase 'māthluhumā'
The phrase 'someone like themselves' indicates that the agent must be of the same legal category. A free adult appoints a free adult; an authorized slave appoints another authorized slave.
This ensures the agent has the capacity to perform the delegated tasks.
3
Derive the condition for valid agency
The principal must have the capacity to perform the act himself (either by adulthood/freedom or by permission), and the agent must share that capacity.
A slave without authorization (ghayr ma'dhun) cannot be a principal because he lacks transactional capacity.
4
Consider the underlying rationale
The Hanafi school allows this based on practical need, as illustrated by the hadith of Abu Tayba, where the Prophet ﷺ permitted a slave to engage in cupping and collect payment, implying agency.
Need (haja) permits delegation even by those with limited capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا وكل الحر البالغ او الماذون مثلهما جاز
And if a free adult or an authorized (slave) appoints someone like themselves, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage explains that it is valid for two categories of people to appoint agents: the free adult (al-hurr al-baligh) and the authorized slave or child (al-ma'dhun). The phrase 'someone like themselves' indicates that the person being appointed as agent should be of the same category: a free adult can appoint a free adult, and a person given permission to trade (ma'dhun) can appoint another ma'dhun. The commentary clarifies that a slave who is authorized by his master can trade and can also appoint an agent for trade. Similarly, a child given permission by his guardian can do the same. This is based on the practical need for such appointments, as shown in the hadith of Abu Tayba (the cupper).

Key Terms
الماذون (al-ma'dhūn): A person (slave or child) who has been given permission by his master or guardian to engage in trade and financial transactions.
الحر البالغ (al-hurr al-baligh): A free adult who has reached puberty and is of sound mind.
مثلهما (māthluhumā): Like them, i.e., of the same legal category: free adult for free adult, ma'dhun for ma'dhun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Any free adult
C) Another ma'dhun
D) Anyone

Key Takeaway
A free adult and an authorized slave (ma'dhun) may each appoint an agent of their own kind to conduct trade.

Appointing a Discerning Child as Agent
Ruling that a child who understands sale and purchase can be appointed as agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on appointing a discerning child as agent and identify who bears liability.

Core Idea
A discerning child under interdiction can act as agent – the principal, not the child, bears all liability.

Can you send your young child to the market to buy groceries? According to the Hanafi school, yes, as long as the child understands buying and selling. This lesson examines the ruling that a child under legal interdiction (mahjur) but possessing discernment (tamyiz) may be appointed as an agent, with all rights and obligations binding the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The passage describes a principal (the appointor) and a child agent. The act is appointing the child to conduct a sale or purchase on behalf of the principal.
The child is described as 'mahjur' (interdicted) but 'ya'qil al-bay' wa al-shira' (understands sale and purchase). This is the key condition.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that such an appointment is permissible (ja'iz). The transaction is valid and its effects attach to the principal.
Permissibility here means the act is legally allowed and produces its intended consequences.
3
Analyze the condition of the agent
The child must be discerning (mumayyiz) – i.e., able to understand the nature of the transaction. The Hanafi school does not require full legal capacity (rushd) for the agent; understanding suffices.
Discerning children can form intent and execute tasks, even if they cannot own property independently.
4
Determine liability
The principal is fully liable for the transaction. The child agent bears no personal responsibility. This is because the child acts as a mere conduit for the principal's will.
If the child errs, the principal may be responsible unless the child exceeded her instructions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان وكلا صبيا محجورا يعقل البيع والشراء
And if one appoints a child who is non-disposable (mahjur) who understands sale and purchase (as agent), it is permissible.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage rules on appointing a child who is not authorized to trade (mahjur 'alayh, i.e., under legal interdiction) but who understands sale and purchase. The child (sabi) here is one who has reached the age of discernment (tamyiz) but has not been given permission by his guardian. The Hanafi school permits such a child to act as an agent for the principal, and the rights and obligations of the transaction bind the principal (the one who appointed him), not the child. This is because the child is not legally responsible, but his understanding makes him capable of executing the transaction on behalf of the principal. The commentary explains that for small tasks, it is common practice to send a discerning child to buy something from the shop. Thus, the agency is valid, but the child is not personally liable.

Key Terms
صبيا محجورا (ṣabiyyan maḥjūran): A child who is legally interdicted from disposing of property (i.e., does not have independent financial capacity).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The child's guardian
C) The principal (the one who appointed the child)
D) The child and the principal jointly

Key Takeaway
A discerning child under interdiction can be an agent; the transaction binds the principal, not the child.

The Agent Must Understand and Intend the Transaction
Ruling on the prerequisite of capacity and intention for a valid agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that an agent must be legally capable (adult and sane) and intend the transaction, and apply it to identify valid agents in agency contracts.

Core Idea
An agent must be legally capable and genuinely intend the transaction.

In Hanafi fiqh, an agency contract requires both principal and agent to have legal capacity. This passage establishes that the agent must understand the transaction and intend it, drawing on the prophetic hadith that exempts children and the insane from legal responsibility. Let us examine the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirement for the agent
The passage states ‘والوكيل ممن يعقل البيع ويقصده’ – the agent must be one who understands the sale and intends it. Determine what this means in terms of legal capacity.
Understanding (al-ʻaql) and intention (al-qasd) are two components.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author refers to the hadith ‘رفع القلم عن ثلاثة’ (the pen is lifted from three). Identify the three categories exempted.
The hadith is found in Sunan Abi Dawud and others.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah (legal implication)
How does this hadith prove that an agent must be an adult of sound mind? The hadith lifts legal responsibility from children and the insane; therefore, their transactions are invalid, and they cannot act as agents.
The exemption covers all legal acts, including agency.
4
Apply the rule to the principal
The passage also notes that if the principal is a child or insane, he cannot appoint an agent. Reflect on the symmetry: both parties must have capacity.
The principal's incapacity also invalidates the agency.
5
Note cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools allow a discerning minor as an agent. The Hanafi school insists on majority and sanity. This difference stems from how the hadith is interpreted.
The Hanafi position is more restrictive.

Source Text

This passage states the ruling on the capacity required of an agent in agency contracts. The commentary further explains that the agent must be an adult of sound mind who grasps the nature of the contract, so that he is not merely playing. The consequence is that one who cannot perform an act cannot delegate it to another.

Validity of Agency: Principal and Agent Must Have Similar Capacity
Ruling with proof on agency capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid agency appointment (wakāla) and identify the evidence from the hadith of Abu Taybah.

Core Idea
Agency is valid when both principal and agent have the legal capacity to transact themselves.

This passage establishes a key principle in Hanafi contract law: an agent can only be appointed by someone who is legally capable of performing the act themselves. The ruling clarifies that a free adult, as well as a mādhūn (a slave or child authorized to trade), can appoint an agent of the same capacity. We will explore the reasoning and the prophetic proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Read the matn: 'If a free adult or a mādhūn appoints someone like them as an agent, it is valid.' The condition is that the principal and agent must share the same legal capacity for the transaction.
This is a specific validation for agency, not a general rule.
2
Define mādhūn
The commentary defines mādhūn as a slave or child who has been given explicit permission by the master or guardian to engage in trade. Understand that this permission grants limited contractual capacity.
Mādhūn is a technical term in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the evidence from hadith
The hadith mentions that the Prophet (ﷺ) hired Abu Taybah (a slave) for cupping and compensated him. This shows that a slave with permission can act as an agent in transactions.
The hadith is from Sahih Bukhari and is used by analogy (qiyas) for agency.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools may not allow a slave to appoint an agent unless the master explicitly permits it. The Hanafi school considers the permission to trade as sufficient authorization.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on differing interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

1377 (8) And if a free adult or a mādhūn appoints someone like them as an agent, it is valid. (1377) (8) If a free adult or a mādhūn appoints someone like themselves as an agent, it is valid. Mādhūn refers to a slave or child who has been given permission by the master or guardian to engage in buying and selling. Therefore, if a free adult appoints an agent, or a slave who has been given permission to trade, or a child who has been given permission to trade, appoints someone as an agent for buying and selling, it is valid. A slave who is an adult of sound mind may be prevented from trading only to avoid harm to the master, but if permission is given, he can buy and sell himself and also appoint an agent. The same applies to a discerning child whose guardian allows him minor transactions; he can buy and sell and appoint an agent. The appointment of a mādhūn slave as agent is indicated in the hadith: Ibn Malik said that Abu Taybah cupped the Messenger of Allah, so he ordered for him a sa' of dates and ordered his family to reduce his tax (Bukhari, chapter on cupping, p. 283 no. 2102). In this hadith, Abu Taybah was a slave and he was permitted to trade.

This passage explains the validity of agency when the principal and agent share similar legal capacity. The ruling states that a free adult or a mādhūn (a slave or child authorized to trade) can appoint an agent of the same category. The commentary clarifies that 'mādhūn' refers to a slave or child who has been given explicit permission by the master or guardian to engage in commerce. The rationale is that since such a person can transact on his own behalf, he can also delegate that authority to another. The hadith of Abu Taybah illustrates that even a slave with trading permission can act as an agent (since the Prophet hired him for cupping, which implies agency).

Key Terms
مأذون (mādhūn): A slave or minor who has been given permission by the master or guardian to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Any slave who is of sound mind
D) A child above the age of puberty

Key Takeaway
Agency is valid when both principal and agent have the legal capacity to perform the transaction, and a mādhūn (authorized slave/child) can both transact and appoint an agent.

Contracts Attributed to the Agent: Rights Attach to the Agent
Classification of agency contracts and the ruling with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding contracts attributed to oneself by an agent and apply the proof from the hadith of Bilal.

Core Idea
When an agent contracts in his own name, he alone bears all rights and obligations—the principal is not liable.

Agency (wakalah) is a foundational contract in Hanafi fiqh. A key distinction arises based on how the agent phrases the contract: if he attributes it to himself, he becomes personally liable. This lesson clarifies the legal consequence and its scriptural basis, ensuring you correctly assign liability in agency transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of agency contracts
The author states that contracts concluded by agents are of two types: those attributed to the agent himself and those attributed to the principal. Focus on the first type.
The second type is covered in a later lesson.
2
Understand the legal consequence
When the agent attributes the contract to himself (e.g., 'I sell'), all rights and obligations—delivery, payment, warranty claims—attach to the agent, not the principal.
The agent becomes the party to the contract.
3
Examine the examples
The text gives examples: the agent delivers the item, receives payment, is sued for defects, etc. These illustrate the rule.
4
Analyze the dalil
The hadith of Bilal: Bilal took a loan in his own name for the Prophet, and the Jew demanded repayment from Bilal, not the Prophet. This proves that when the agent contracts in his own name, he bears the obligations.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for establishing the ruling.

حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت جابر اور حضرت عامر دونوں سے مروی ہے کہ غلام کے لئے کوئی كفالت نہیں ہے۔
1379 (10) And the contracts that agents conclude are of two types. Every contract that the agent attributes to himself, such as sale, purchase, and lease, the rights of that contract attach to the agent and not the principal. Thus, he delivers the sold item and receives the price. (repeated from previous) ... (1379) (10) The contracts that agents conclude are of two types. Every contract that the agent attributes to himself, for example, sale, purchase, and lease, the rights of those contracts attach to the agent, not the principal. Therefore, he will deliver the sold item, he will take possession of the price. He will be the one from whom the price is demanded when he buys something, and he will take possession of the property, and he will be the one who disputes over defects. In agency, he attributes the contract to himself and not to the principal; he says, 'I sell', 'I buy', or 'I lease'. In such contracts, all rights attach to the agent, not to the principal. Examples are given in the text: the agent delivers the sold item to the buyer, the agent receives the price. If the agent buys something, the price is demanded from him. If a defect appears in the sold item, the agent is the party in the lawsuit. (1) He is the contractor and he attributed the contract to himself, so he is responsible for the rights. (2) A long hadith mentions that Bilal took a loan from a Jew for the Prophet, and the Jew demanded it from Bilal, and Bilal repaid it. The fragment of the long hadith: 'Hadathani 'Abdullah al-Hawzani...'
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage introduces the classification of contracts in agency. The author states that contracts are of two types: those attributed by the agent to himself (like 'I sell') and those attributed to the principal. The focus here is on the first type: when the agent names himself as the party, all rights and obligations (such as delivery, payment, and warranty claims) attach to the agent, not the principal. The commentary provides examples: the agent must deliver the goods, receive payment, and be the one sued for defects. The proof is the hadith of Bilal, who took a loan in his own name for the Prophet, and the creditor demanded repayment from Bilal, not the Prophet.

Key Terms
عقد يضيفه الوكيل إلى نفسه (ʻaqd yudīfuhu al-wakīlu ilā nafsihi): A contract where the agent expresses the transaction as his own (e.g., 'I sell').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hadith of Bilal, who demanded repayment from Bilal?
A) The Prophet
B) The Jew lender
C) Abu Bakr
D) The principal

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when an agent attributes a contract to himself, he becomes solely responsible for its rights and duties.

intermediate
8 min
Two Types of Agency Contracts and Their Effects
Classification of agency contracts based on attribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of agency contracts based on attribution and state which party bears the rights and obligations in each type.

Core Idea
In agency, who bears the contract's rights depends on how the agent attributes the contract: to himself or to the principal.

Agency (wakalah) allows one person to act on behalf of another. However, the legal effects of the contract depend on how the agent expresses the transaction. This lesson examines the Hanafi classification of agency contracts into two types: those where the agent attributes the contract to himself, and those where he attributes it to the principal. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining who is liable for the contract's obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of agency contracts
The passage divides contracts made by agents into two categories based on attribution. First, identify each type: (1) the agent attributes the contract to himself (e.g., 'I sell'), and (2) the agent attributes the contract to the principal (e.g., 'I marry you to so-and-so').
The key is the wording used by the agent.
2
Determine the legal effect for each type
For type 1, rights and obligations (delivery, payment, litigation) attach to the agent. For type 2, they attach to the principal. Note that the agent in the second type is merely a messenger (safir).
Examples: sale vs. marriage.
3
Examine the evidence for type 1
The author cites the hadith of Bilal, who took a loan as the Prophet's agent and was demanded repayment personally. This shows that when the agent attributes the contract to himself, he bears liability.
Hadith from Sunan al-Bayhaqi.
4
Examine the evidence for type 2
The author cites the hadith of Umm Salamah appointing her minor son as agent for her marriage, where rights attached to her, not the son. Also the hadith of the Prophet acting as an agent for a companion, where the companion was responsible for the mahr.
Hadith from Nasa'i and Bukhari.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The passage indicates that the Shafi'i school may treat some contracts differently (e.g., marriage). Understand that the Hanafi position is based on the principle of attribution.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate.
